1.0          INTRODUCTION AND PROJECT AREA
The City of Walnut Creek, in conjunction with Contra Costa County and Caltrans,
received a Program for Arterial System Synchronization (PASS) grant from the
Metropolitan Transportation Commission (MTC) to conduct a signal timing study for
twenty-seven (27) traffic signals on Ygnacio Valley Road and Treat Boulevard, with one
of the signals on N. Main Street. The goal of the project was to conduct timing analysis
and develop and implement new weekday and weekend signal coordination plans and
to implement traffic responsive timing during off-peak periods. Three (3) of the signals
are within Contra Costa County and four (4) of the signals are within Caltrans right-of-
way, but all of the signals are operated and maintained by the City of Walnut Creek.
Table 1 summarizes the project intersections and scope of work for each and Figure 1,
on page 3, illustrates the study area.
                                Table 1: Project Intersections

Traffic responsive timing is a method of providing signal coordination by enabling signal
timing plans through actual traffic volumes along the corridor as opposed to plans
being enabled at specific times during the day. Traffic volumes and loop detector
occupancy data are continuously measured along the corridor and then a specific
coordination plan selected from a “bank” of plans based on those volumes and
occupancy. Traffic responsive operation allows the system to select the most
appropriate plan based on the actual traffic conditions and respond to daily, weekly, and
monthly traffic fluctuations. Therefore, coordination operates during only those times at
which it is needed and with the most appropriate timing plan, resulting in reduced
delay, vehicle emissions, and improved safety. For this project, new peak hour timing
patterns were prepared and then used as the basis for development of the various
timing patterns for traffic responsive operations. Based on City preference, the peak
period coordination plans continue to operate during the weekday AM and PM peak
periods under time-of-day operations. For the other periods of the day, during off-
peak, midday, and weekend periods, the signals operate in traffic responsive mode.
An analysis of existing conditions was completed and existing conditions models were
prepared to be used as the basis for developing the proposed signal timing plans.
Information regarding the existing conditions was presented in the Final Existing
Conditions Report. Once the existing conditions were established, the next step in the
timing development process consisted of an evaluation of the signal grouping and cycle

PASS – 2014/15 City of Walnut Creek                                                     Page 2
Final Project Report                                                                 July 2015
lengths for the project intersections. Kimley-Horn met with the City and provided
interim recommendations regarding the signal grouping and cycle lengths for the peak
period plans. Once the cycle lengths were selected, detailed recommended timing
plans, to include a detailed review of splits and offsets, were completed for the peak
period plans. Using the peak period plans, additional patterns were developed for use
in traffic responsive mode and other traffic responsive parameters selected. Time of day
patterns and traffic responsive patterns were then implemented and fine-tuned by
Kimley-Horn staff. This report provides a summary of the project and includes an
evaluation of the project through review of the “Before” and “After” travel time studies.

2.0        E XISTING CONDITIONS REVIEW
The initial phase of the project included collecting and reviewing existing conditions
data to enable development and evaluation of the traffic responsive plans. Data
collection included the following:
    §   Collect detector data and existing timing plan information
    §   Collect turning movement counts
    §   Conduct travel time and delay studies along the project corridors
    §   Collect collision history
    §   Develop existing conditions model

   2.1        Traffic Volume Data
Turning movement counts, including vehicular, pedestrian, and bicycle counts, were
collected at the twenty-seven (27) intersections as shown in Table 1. Turning
movement counts were collected for six (6) hours during the following times:
    §   AM Peak               7:00 AM to 9:00 AM
    §   Midday Peak           11:30 AM to 1:30 PM
    §   PM Peak               4:00 PM to 6:00 PM
Turning movement counts, including vehicular, pedestrian, and bicycle counts, were
collected during the midday peak period on one Saturday at the eight (8) signals as
shown in Table 1. The turning movement counts were collected for 2-hours during the
following times:
    §   Saturday Midday Peak          1:00 PM to 3:00 PM
Weekday turning movement counts were collected on Thursday, October 9 th, 2014.
Weekend turning movement counts were collected on Saturday, October 11 th, 2014.
The turning movement counts were collected using video cameras and then reduced in
the office. Copies of the turning movement counts were sent electronically to the City
separately.
To understand daily and weekday traffic volumes along the corridors, 24-hour tube
counts were collected for seven days at the following five (5) locations:
    §   Ygnacio Valley Road between Oak Grove Road and Via Monte
    §   Ygnacio Valley Road between Bancroft Road and San Carlos Drive
    §   Ygnacio Valley Road between Civic and Broadway
    §   Treat Boulevard between Oak Road and Jones Road
    §   Treat Boulevard between Bancroft Road and Candelero Drive



PASS – 2014/15 City of Walnut Creek                                             Page 5
Final Project Report                                                          July 2015
24-hour tube counts were collected for seven (7) days between Saturday, November 1,
2014 and Friday, November 7, 2014.
In addition, the City provided 24-hour system counts (@15 minutes intervals) in Excel
format at key locations along the project corridors to assist with development of traffic
responsive parameters.

   2.2          “Before” Travel Time Survey
To assist with evaluation of the project, “Before” implementation floating vehicle surveys
were conducted along the project corridors. The travel time surveys were conducted
during each respective corridor’s peak period with an additional goal to capture the
travel time and delay conditions for the periods at which it was anticipated for different
off-peak traffic responsive timing to run.
The weekday floating car travel time surveys were conducted on Wednesday, October
22nd, 2014 between 7:00 AM and 7:00 PM, the weekend floating car travel time runs
were conducted on Saturday, November 1 st 2014 between 9:30 AM and 6:00 PM. The
travel time surveys were conducted along the corridors with the following limits:
      § Main Street between Sunnyvale Avenue and Treat Boulevard
      § Treat Boulevard between Main Street and Carriage Drive
      § Ygnacio Valley Road between Oak Grove Road and SB I-680 Ramps
The results of the “Before” study for each corridor and peak period are summarized
below in Tables 2 through 5. Detailed travel time summaries for the corridors are
included in the Appendix.

   2.3        Existing Conditions Traffic Models
Synchro models for the Ygnacio Valley Road and Treat Boulevard corridors were
provided to Kimley-Horn along the project corridors with past signal re-timing projects
by the City. As a result, those models previously developed were used as the basis for
development of the traffic responsive plans. Based on the current timing sheets
provided by the City, Kimley-Horn reviewed and updated the models to incorporate the
new traffic volumes and changes in the signal timing parameters since the re-timing was
last conducted.
The models were calibrated by verifying that the data, such as queue lengths, degree of
saturation, average delay, and travel time reasonably matched the observed conditions
in the field. Adjustments to input data such as saturation flow rates, travel speeds,
traffic volumes, and lane geometry (i.e. locations where vehicles use a wide single lane
as two lanes), were reviewed to achieve a reasonable match between the model and
observed conditions.




PASS – 2014/15 City of Walnut Creek                                               Page 8
Final Project Report                                                            July 2015
3.0        TRAFFIC RESPONSIVE TIMING DEVELOPMENT
Once the existing conditions were established, the next step in the process was to
develop new timing patterns and traffic responsive parameters. The traffic responsive
analysis and timing plan development included the following steps:
    §   Evaluating and establishing signal groups
    §   Review of existing patterns and development of additional patterns
    §   Development of responsive parameters and threshold values
    §   Determining time of operation for traffic responsive

   3.1        Signal Grouping
Once the data was collected, the volumes, traffic patterns, and existing timing plans
were reviewed to determine signal groups for operation of the traffic responsive system.
Based on the existing timing plans that are in operation and the close spacing of the
traffic signals, it was determined that both corridors, Ygnacio Valley Road and Treat
Boulevard, would each operate as a single group for traffic responsive operation similar
to existing operation. It should be noted, some intersections within the group operate
with different cycle lengths or operate free under certain patterns. The system
determines the pattern that each of the signals in the group will operate, with the
pattern set-up at each intersection dictating the cycle length. As a result, signals that
will operate with a different cycle length or in some cases free under certain conditions,
will be done so by pattern not by changing the grouping.

   3.2        Pattern Development and Pattern Matrix
The Naztec system allows for up to six (6) cycle lengths. As a result, the system can have
six (6) varying cycle lengths, which provides a good selection to adequately adjust
varying changes in daily traffic patterns. A review of the existing patterns and cycle
length was conducted to determine what cycle lengths would be used for the analysis.
In addition to having varying cycle lengths, the patterns were set for varying directional
preference (inbound vs. outbound). For the analysis and deployment, inbound was
considered the peak AM period direction and outbound was considered the peak PM
peak direction.

Based on the existing patterns and the need for patterns with varying
inbound/balanced/outbound preference, additional plans were developed to complete
the responsive pattern matrix. Each of the plans were developed based on the
proposed time of day plans and adjusted accordingly based on varying volumes. As an
example, if a lower cycle inbound plan was needed for lower volume levels, the AM plan,

PASS – 2014/15 City of Walnut Creek                                                 Page 9
Final Project Report                                                              July 2015
which has inbound preference, was used by lowering the volume using the growth
factor feature in the model. The tables summarizing the patterns for all groups are
included in the Appendix A-2.

   3.1        Time of Operation
Different masters and pattern matrices were set up for each period, the AM period, the
PM period, Off-peak period (includes midday), and Weekend period. Time of operation
was reviewed and adjusted separately for each corridor based on traffic conditions and
field observations. See Table 6 for the recommended time of operation before fine-
tuning.

4.0        FINE-TUNING TIMING CHANGES
Once the timing patterns and responsive parameters were developed, Kimley-Horn
input the timings into the system The initial fine-tuning process consisted deploying the
new patterns by time-of-day and fine-tuning of each pattern in the field. New pattern
fine-tuning consisted of reviewing splits and offsets and making adjustments based on
field conditions.
Once the patterns were fine-tuned, the traffic responsive system was fine-tuned. Initial
fine-tuning of the responsive system included bench testing the system by running the
operation in the office without deploying it in the field. During the office fine-tuning,
various responsive parameters were adjusted, such as threshold values, to calibrate the
system prior to field deployment. Initial office fine-tuning was completed by Kimley-
Horn.
Once the system was calibrated in the office, the system was deployed in the field. The
fine-tuning primarily consisted of both field observations and watching the operations
from the City Traffic Management Center (TMC) by monitoring how the system was
deploying patterns and adjusting settings to have proper plans in place based on
volume conditions. Thresholds and pattern matrix adjustments along with time of
operation changes were made based on the plan deployment and field conditions.
The following highlight typical fine-tuning adjustments that occurred:
    §   Cycle length adjustment of time based coordination plans
    §   Time of operation of traffic responsive masters
    §   Split and offset adjustments
    §   Broken sample detectors removed from the system
    §   The pattern numbers were adjusted for better organization in the system
    §   All threshold values were adjusted
    §   The pattern matrix was updated for each system.
The final pattern matrices for each system and timing sheet summaries for each new
pattern in operation after fine-tuning are included in the Appendix A-3. Table 7
displays the final fine-tuned time of operation of the various traffic responsive masters
and time of day patterns.

5.0         EVALUATION
A traditional comparison of the “Before” vs. “After” travel time runs was completed for
evaluating the benefits of the project. The following sections summarize the typical
operation and the evaluation.
   5.1        “Before” vs. “After” Field Travel Time Comparison
An “after” implementation floating vehicle survey was conducted during the periods of
day where the new signal coordination plans were implemented to evaluate the project
timing plans. The survey was conducted during the following peak periods and
corresponding times:
    §    Weekday AM Peak:                7:00 AM – 10:00 AM
    §    Weekday Midday Peak:            10:45 AM – 2:00 PM
    §    Weekday PM Peak:                3:00 PM – 7:00 PM
    §    Weekend AM Peak:                9:30 AM – 11:30 AM

   5.2        Benefit-Cost Analysis
To understand the signal timing benefits, a benefit-cost analysis was conducted using the
Metropolitan Transportation Commission (MTC) method and spreadsheet analysis tool
and before and after travel time comparison. The total benefits, in dollars, were
calculated for the annual average and for a five-year project life cycle based on the total
yearly travel time savings, and resultant time, fuel, and emission reductions. In addition,
total project costs were calculated to include the City staff time dedicated to the project
and consultant costs for development of the timings and completion of the study. The
initial capital costs to purchase the traffic responsive system module and training
associated with the module had been incorporated into previous projects, therefore were
not included in this project.
In addition to the benefits from the vehicular travel time and delays, there was a
reduction in vehicle emissions. Emission reductions in reactive organic gases and
nitrogen oxides are associated with increasing average traffic speeds to up to 40 mph.
Particulate matter reduction can also be tied to an increase in average speed, but are
less dependent on this variable. To measure the reduction of each of these pollutants,
the California Environmental Protection Agency Air Resources Board (ARB) uses
calculated emission factors. Emission factors are dependent on the before project and
after project average traffic speeds.

A key component of the benefit cost analysis is the traffic volume on the corridor during
the period of analysis. Since coordination plans are active during off-peak periods,
when volumes were not collected, traffic volumes needed to be adjusted to better
represent the actual traffic conditions during the periods studied. To estimate the
volume during the off-peak periods, the peak hour turning movement counts were
factored based on the daily tube count volumes collected along the corridor.
A summary of some of the key findings from the results of the benefit-cost analysis is as
follows:
    § Average reduction in travel time – 12%
    § Average speed increase – 14%
    § Average fuel savings – 8%
    § Average reduction in auto signal delay – 32%
PASS – 2014/15 City of Walnut Creek                                                Page 16
Final Project Report                                                              July 2015
    §   Average reduction in stops – 25%
    §   The results of the analysis showed a total 5-year lifetime travel time savings of
        approximately $10,070,446 and fuel consumption savings of approximately
        $4,133,601.
    §   Total cost of the projects including the consultant cost, agency staff costs, and
        maintenance cost is approximately $95,981.
    §   The project obtained a benefit/cost ratio of nearly 105:1.

The summary on the next page shows the total project savings and total project costs,
and the resultant benefit-cost ratio. The table also summarizes the assumptions and
methods for calculating the project benefits and costs. In addition, a detailed summary
of the overall project benefit cost as well as individual summaries for each corridor
separately are included in Appendix A-5.

4, page 28, June 1997. Adjusted for inflation using Consumer Price Index (CPI), from US Dept of Labor, Bureau of Labor Statistics, CPI - All Urban
Consumers, San Francisco-Oakland-San Jose, CA area, All Items, Not Seasonally Adjusted (Series Id:CUURA422SA0). Vehicle fleet assumed to be

for year 2, 30% for year 3, and 10% for year 4.

   5.3        Other Benefits
Other benefits were achieved by the project that are not included in the benefit-cost
analysis, based primarily on the travel time savings and reduced side street delay along
the corridors. Traffic responsive operation allows the system to select the most
appropriate plan based on the actual traffic conditions and respond to daily, weekly, and
monthly traffic fluctuations. Since the traffic responsive operation will be in place during
periods with varying volume conditions, such as during different times of the year where
traffic is lighter (holidays or summer) or periods where traffic is heaver such as when
incidents occur on the freeway and traffic diverts to the corridor, additional benefits
could be observed since more appropriate timing plans would be deployed.
Coordination plans could operate during only those times at which it is needed and with
the most appropriate timing plan, resulting in reduced delay, vehicle emissions, and
improved safety. Lastly, existing pedestrian and bicycle timing were maintained with all
patterns that operate with the traffic responsive timing. Therefore, the implementation
of traffic responsive does not have a negative impact on pedestrian and bicycle timing
and in some cases pedestrian and bicycle delay is reduced when lower cycle lengths are
selected during lighter traffic periods.
